Now, we need to determine all possible a1 such that the sequence is infinite. That means we need to find a1 such that the process never fails (i.e., every a_n has at least three proper divisors). Now, we need to find all a1 that lead to an infinite sequence. That is, the orbit never hits a number with less than 3 proper divisors. Since we have found many numbers that work (fixed points and some preimages), we need to determine the complete set.

Observation: The process is defined by a_{n+1} = sum of three largest proper divisors of a_n. Let's denote f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N. For N with at least 3 proper divisors, f(N) is defined. We need to find all N such that the sequence N, f(N), f(f(N)), ... never hits a number with less than 3 proper divisors. This is like the set of numbers that are in the "basin of attraction" of the fixed points (or maybe other cycles). We have found fixed points F = {6k: k odd, 5 ∤ k}. Also we have numbers that map to these fixed points, like 72, 216, 648, etc. Are there numbers that map to these fixed points after more than one step? For example, 72 maps to 78 (fixed). 216 maps to 234 (fixed). 648 maps to 702 (fixed). So these are preimages of fixed points. Could there be numbers that map to these preimages? For instance, is there a number N such that f(N)=72? Then N would be a preimage of 72, and then the sequence would be N,72,78,78,... So N would work if f(N)=72 and 72 works. So we need to find all N such that f(N)=72. Similarly, for 216, etc. So we need to characterize the set of numbers that eventually reach a fixed point. Possibly all numbers that are multiples of 6? But 30 fails. So maybe numbers that are not divisible by 5? But 12 is divisible by 3 and 4, and fails. So it's more subtle.

Let's try to analyze f(N) more systematically. For a positive integer N, let its prime factorization be N = ∏ p_i^{e_i}. The divisors are all numbers of the form ∏ p_i^{a_i} with 0 ≤ a_i ≤ e_i. The largest proper divisor is N/p_min where p_min is the smallest prime factor. The second largest proper divisor: we need to consider the next largest divisor less than N. Typically, it will be N/p_2 where p_2 is the second smallest prime factor (if p_min^2 does not divide N) or N/p_min^2 if p_min^2 divides N and p_min^2 < p_2? But careful: For numbers with repeated prime factors, the divisors can be N/p_min^2, N/p_min^3, etc. The three largest proper divisors are the three largest numbers d < N such that d|N. Since the divisors are symmetric: if d divides N, then N/d also divides N. The largest proper divisor is N/p_min. The second largest is either N/p_2 (if p_2 is the second smallest prime factor) or N/p_min^2 (if p_min^2 divides N and p_min^2 < p_2? Actually compare N/p_min^2 and N/p_2: N/p_min^2 > N/p_2 iff p_min^2 < p_2. Since p_min is the smallest prime, p_min^2 could be less than p_2 if p_min=2 and p_2=5? 4<5, so N/4 > N/5. So if N is divisible by 4, then N/4 is a divisor and is larger than N/5. So second largest could be N/4. But also N/3 is larger than N/4? Actually N/3 > N/4. So order: N/2 > N/3 > N/4 > N/5 > N/6 ... So the second largest is N/3 if 3|N, else N/4 if 4|N and 3∤N? Actually if N is divisible by 2 and 3, then N/2 and N/3 are both divisors, and N/2 > N/3. So second largest is N/3. If N is divisible by 2 but not by 3, then the next largest divisor could be N/4 if 4|N, or N/5 if 5|N? But also N/2 is largest, so we need to compare N/4 and N/5: N/4 > N/5. So if N is divisible by 4, then N/4 is a divisor and is > N/5. So second largest is N/4. If N is divisible by 2 but not by 4, then N/2 is largest, and the next largest divisor could be N/3 if 3|N? But if 3∤N, then the next candidate is N/5 if 5|N, etc. But also N/ (p) for p>2. So the three largest proper divisors are: the three largest numbers among {N/p : p is a prime factor of N} ∪ {N/p^2 : p^2|N} ∪ {N/p^3 : p^3|N} ... but note that N/p^2 is a divisor if p^2|N. However, N/p^2 may be less than N/p for p>1. So the three largest are typically: N/p_min, then either N/p_2 or N/p_min^2, then the next largest among the remaining.

But we can compute f(N) by considering the set of divisors. There is known concept: For a composite number N, the sum of the three largest proper divisors is often N + something? Not sure.

Proper:1,2,3,4,6,7,8,9,12,14,18,21,24,28,36,42,56,63,72,84,126,168,252. Three largest:252,168,126 sum=546. s(504)=546. 546=2*3*7*13. Divisors:1,2,3,6,7,13,14,21,26,39,42,78,91,182,273,546. Proper:1,2,3,6,7,13,14,21,26,39,42,78,91,182,273. Three largest:273,182,91 sum=546. So 546 is fixed? Check: 546/2=273, 546/3=182, 546/6=91. Yes, 546 is fixed. So 504 works! So 504=2^3*3^2*7 works. So not just 2^3*3^k; also 2^3*3^2*7 works. Similarly, 2^3*3^2*13=2^3*3^2*13=8*9*13=936? Let's test 936: 936=2^3*3^2*13. Divisors: 1,2,3,4,6,8,9,12,13,18,24,26,36,39,52,54,78,104,117,156,182,216,312,468,936? Actually compute: 936=8*9*13=2^3*3^2*13. Divisors: all combinations of 2^a,3^b,13^c with a=0..3, b=0..2, c=0..1. The largest proper divisor: N/2=468. Next: N/3=312. Next: N/6=156. So s(936)=468+312+156=936. So 936 is fixed! So 936 works. So 2^3*3^2*13 works. So it seems that numbers of the form 2^3 * 3^2 * p where p is a prime >3? But 2^3*3^2*5=360 fails? Actually we tested 360 and it failed. So p=5 fails. Why? Because for 360, the three largest proper divisors are not 180,120,90? Actually 360=2^3*3^2*5. Divisors: 1,2,3,4,5,6,8,9,10,12,15,18,20,24,30,36,40,45,60,72,90,120,180,360. The three largest proper: 180,120,90 sum=390. So s(360)=390, not 360. So 360 is not fixed. But 360 maps to 390, which is not fixed. So 360 fails. So why does 504 work? 504=2^3*3^2*7. Its three largest proper divisors: 252,168,126 sum=546. So s(504)=546, which is fixed. So 504 works because its f-image is a fixed point. So the condition for a number N to work is that f(N) is in F. So we need to find all N such that f(N) ∈ F. This is a kind of preimage problem.

Maybe we can characterize the set of numbers that eventually map to a fixed point. Could it be that all numbers that are not divisible by 5 and not of the form 2^a * 3^b * something? Let's try to see if there is a pattern: The fixed points are numbers N such that N ≡ 6 mod 12 and 5 ∤ N. So they are numbers that are 6 times an odd number not divisible by 5. So they are numbers of the form 6m where m is odd and 5∤m. So F = {6m: m odd, 5∤m}. Now, we need to find all N such that f(N) ∈ F. Since f(N) is defined as sum of three largest proper divisors, we can try to compute f(N) in terms of N's factorization.

Observation: For any composite number N, the sum of its three largest proper divisors is at most N + something? Actually, we can bound f(N). Since the largest proper divisor is N/p_min, and the second largest is at most N/3 (if 3|N) or N/4, etc. So f(N) ≤ N/2 + N/3 + N/6 = N, with equality if and only if the three largest are exactly N/2, N/3, N/6. That is the condition for f(N)=N. So for numbers that are not fixed points, f(N) may be greater than N or less than N? Let's test: For N=12, f(12)=13 >12. For N=30, f(30)=31 >30. For N=36, f(36)=39 >36. For N=48, f(48)=52 >48. For N=60, f(60)=65 >60. For N=84, f(84)=91 >84. For N=90, f(90)=93 >90. For N=120, f(120)=130 >120. For N=132, f(132)=143 >132. For N=144, f(144)=156 >144. For N=156, f(156)=169 >156. For N=168, f(168)=182 >168. For N=180, f(180)=195 >180. For N=192, f(192)=208 >192. For N=204, f(204)=221 >204. For N=210, f(210)=217 >210. For N=228, f(228)=247 >228. For N=240, f(240)=260 >240. For N=252, f(252)=273 >252. For N=264, f(264)=286 >264. For N=270, f(270)=279 >270. For N=276, f(276)=299 >276. For N=288, f(288)=312 >288. For N=300, f(300)=325 >300. For N=312, f(312)=338 >312. For N=324, f(324)=351 >324. For N=336, f(336)=364 >336. So it seems that for many numbers, f(N) > N. For fixed points, f(N)=N. For numbers like 72, f(72)=78 >72. So f(N) is often greater than N. But is it always greater than N for numbers not in F? Possibly yes, because if N is not a fixed point, then the three largest proper divisors are not exactly N/2,N/3,N/6, so the sum is > N? Let's check: For N=6, f(6)=6, fixed. For N=18, f(18)=18, fixed. For N=42, f(42)=42, fixed. For N=54, f(54)=54, fixed. For N=66, f(66)=66, fixed. For N=78, f(78)=78, fixed. For N=102, f(102)=102, fixed. For N=114, f(114)=114, fixed. For N=126, f(126)=126, fixed. For N=138, f(138)=138, fixed. For N=162, f(162)=162, fixed. For N=174, f(174)=174, fixed. For N=186, f(186)=186, fixed. For N=198, f(198)=198, fixed. For N=222, f(222)=222, fixed. For N=234, f(234)=234, fixed. For N=246, f(246)=246, fixed. For N=258, f(258)=258, fixed. For N=282, f(282)=282, fixed. For N=294, f(294)=294, fixed. For N=306, f(306)=306, fixed. For N=342, f(342)=342, fixed. So indeed, for numbers in F, f(N)=N. For numbers not in F, we have seen f(N) > N in all examples. Could it be that f(N) ≥ N always, with equality iff N ∈ F? Let's test N=1? Not allowed. N=4? Not allowed (only 2 proper divisors). N=8? f(8)=7 <8. So for N=8, f(8)=7 <8. So f(N) can be less than N. But 8 is not in F because it doesn't have 3 proper divisors? Actually 8 has proper divisors 1,2,4 => three proper divisors, so it's allowed. And f(8)=7, which is less than 8. So the inequality is not always ≥. So there are numbers where f(N) < N. For example, N=8,10,14,16,20,22,24,26,28,30,32,34,35,36,38,40,44,45,46,48,50,51,52,56,57,58,60,62,63,64,68,70,72,74,76,77,78? 78 is fixed, so f=78. 80? f(80)=76<80. 84? f(84)=91>84. So some numbers have f(N) < N, some > N. So the dynamics is not monotonic.

We need to find all a1 such that the orbit never hits a number with less than 3 proper divisors. Since we have found many numbers that work, we need to characterize them.

Let's try to analyze the function f(N) more systematically. For a given N, let its prime factorization be N = ∏ p_i^{e_i}. The set of divisors is symmetric: d and N/d. The largest proper divisor is N/p_min. The second largest proper divisor: we need to consider the next largest divisor less than N. Since divisors come in pairs, the largest proper divisor is N/p_min. The next largest proper divisor could be either N/p_2 (if p_2 is the second smallest prime factor) or N/p_min^2 (if p_min^2 divides N and p_min^2 < p_2? Actually compare N/p_min^2 and N/p_2: N/p_min^2 > N/p_2 iff p_min^2 < p_2. Since p_min is the smallest prime, p_min^2 could be less than p_2 if p_min=2 and p_2=5? 4<5, so yes. So if N is divisible by 4, then N/4 is a divisor and is larger than N/5. So the second largest divisor is N/3 if 3|N, else N/4 if 4|N and 3∤N, else N/5 if 5|N, etc. But also if N has a prime factor p such that p_min^2 divides N, then N/p_min^2 is a divisor, but it might be less than N/p_2 if p_min^2 > p_2. For example, N=12=2^2*3, p_min=2, p_2=3, N/2=6, N/3=4, N/4=3. So second largest is 4 (N/3). So indeed, the second largest is N/p_2 if p_2 exists (i.e., N has at least 2 distinct primes) and p_2 < p_min^2? Actually p_min^2=4, p_2=3, so 3<4, so N/3 > N/4. So second largest is N/p_2. For N=18=2*3^2, p_min=2, p_2=3, N/2=9, N/3=6, N/6=3. So second largest is 6 (N/3). For N=20=2^2*5, p_min=2, p_2=5, N/2=10, N/4=5, N/5=4. So second largest is 5? Actually divisors:1,2,4,5,10,20. Proper:1,2,4,5,10. Three largest:10,5,4 sum=19. So second largest is 5 (N/4? Wait N/4=5, N/5=4, so second largest is N/4). So here p_2=5, but N/4=5, and N/5=4. So the second largest is N/4, not N/5. So the order: N/2=10, then N/4=5, then N/5=4. So second largest is N/4. So the second largest divisor is N/p where p is the smallest prime factor such that p^2 divides N? Actually p_min=2, and 2^2=4 divides N, so N/4 is a divisor. Compare N/4 and N/5: N/4 > N/5 because 4<5. So second largest is N/4. So the second largest is N/p_min^2 if p_min^2 divides N and p_min^2 < p_2? But here p_2=5, and p_min^2=4 <5, so yes. So the pattern: The three largest proper divisors are: d1 = N/p_min, d2 = max( N/p_2, N/p_min^2 ) if both exist, else the larger of the two, and d3 = the next largest among the remaining. But we can compute f(N) by considering the set of divisors.

Maybe there is a known result: For a composite number N, the sum of its three largest proper divisors is equal to N + something? Let's try to compute f(N) for N that are multiples of 6 but not fixed. For N=30, f=31; 36, f=39; 42 fixed; 48, f=52; 54 fixed; 60, f=65; 66 fixed; 72, f=78; 78 fixed; 84, f=91; 90, f=93; 96, f=104; 102 fixed; 108, f=117; 114 fixed; 120, f=130; 126 fixed; 132, f=143; 138 fixed; 144, f=156; 150, f=155; 156, f=169; 162 fixed; 168, f=182; 174 fixed; 180, f=195; 186 fixed; 192, f=208; 198 fixed; 204, f=221; 210, f=217; 222 fixed; 228, f=247; 234 fixed; 240, f=260; 246 fixed; 252, f=273; 258 fixed; 264, f=286; 270, f=279; 276, f=299; 282 fixed; 288, f=312; 294 fixed; 300, f=325; 306 fixed; 312, f=338; 324, f=351; 330? 330=2*3*5*11. So it seems that numbers that are multiples of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5 are fixed points. Numbers that are multiples of 6 and divisible by 4 but not by 5 may sometimes work (like 72, 216, 504, 936) and sometimes not (like 288, 336, 384, 432? 432=2^4*3^3, let's test 432: 432=2^4*3^3. So why do some numbers work? Let's examine the numbers that work: 72, 216, 504, 936, 648, 2916, etc. These are all numbers that are divisible by 8 and also by 9? 72=8*9, 216=8*27, 504=8*63, 936=8*117, 648=8*81. So it seems that numbers with exponent of 2 exactly 3 (i.e., divisible by 8 but not by 16) and any exponent of 3 (≥2?) might work. But 288 has exponent 5, fails. 336 has exponent 4, fails. 384 has exponent 7, fails. So maybe numbers with exponent of 2 exactly 3 work? Let's test 2^3 * 3^2 * 7 = 504 works. 2^3 * 3^2 * 13 = 936 works. 2^3 * 3^2 * 5 = 360 fails. So why does 5 cause failure? Because 360 has exponent of 2 exactly 3, but fails. So it's not just exponent of 2. For 360, f=390, and 390 is not fixed. So 360 fails. So the condition for a number N to work is that f(N) is in F. For N=360, f=390, and 390=2*3*5*13. Is 390 in F? Maybe we can characterize the set of numbers that eventually map to a fixed point. Could it be that all numbers that are not divisible by 5 and not of the form 2^a * 3^b * something? Now, note that for any N, f(N) is a divisor sum. Maybe we can find an invariant: For a number N, consider its "s(N)" as defined. Possibly the sequence always eventually becomes constant if and only if a1 is not a prime and not a product of two primes? But 8 is product of 2 and 4? Actually 8=2^3, has 3 proper divisors, but fails. So not that.

Let's step back and think about the problem. The problem is from an olympiad (maybe IMO Shortlist). It asks: Determine all possible values of a1. So there must be a nice characterization. Possibly the answer is: a1 is any positive integer that is not a prime and not a product of two primes? But we have counterexamples: 8 is not prime and not product of two primes (since 8=2*4, but 4 is not prime; product of two primes means both factors are primes, so 8 is not product of two primes). Yet 8 fails. So that's not it.

Maybe the answer is: a1 is any composite number that is not a prime power? But 8 is a prime power (2^3) and fails, but 27 is a prime power and fails? 27 gave 13, fails. So prime powers fail. But 6 is not a prime power and works. 18 is not a prime power (2*3^2) and works. 42 works. 54 works. 66 works. 78 works. 102 works. 114 works. 126 works. 138 works. 162 works. 174 works. 186 works. 198 works. 222 works. 234 works. 246 works. 258 works. 282 works. 294 works. 306 works. 342 works. But 8 is composite with only one prime factor, fails. So maybe the condition is that a1 is composite and has at least two distinct prime factors? But 30 has two distinct primes (2,3,5) and fails. So not that.

Maybe the condition is that a1 is not divisible by 5? But 12 is not divisible by 5 and fails. So no.

Let's try to analyze the function f(N) more systematically. We can try to compute f(N) in terms of N's prime factorization. Let N = ∏ p_i^{e_i}. Actually N=72t, t odd, so N is divisible by 72, which is divisible by 12, so yes. So this is a family.

Subcase B: 4∤N but 5|N. Then d3 = N/5. So f(N) = (5N/6) + N/5 = (25N/30 + 6N/30) = (31N/30). So f(N)=31N/30. For f(N) to be integer, N must be divisible by 30. Since N is multiple of 6 and 5|N, N is divisible by 30. So let N=30m. Then f(N)=31m. So we need 31m ∈ F. That is, 31m must be a fixed point. 31m is a fixed point iff 31m is a multiple of 6, not divisible by 4, not divisible by 5. Since 31m is odd if m odd, even if m even. For it to be a multiple of 6, it must be divisible by 2 and 3. So m must be even and divisible by 3. So m=6t. Then 31m=186t. Now, we need 186t not divisible by 4 and not divisible by 5. 186t = 2*3*31*t. For it to be not divisible by 4, we need t odd (since 2*odd is not divisible by 4). Also need 5∤186t, i.e., 5∤t. So t odd and 5∤t. Then N=30m=30*6t=180t, with t odd and 5∤t. So N = 180t where t is odd and not multiple of 5. But wait, we also need that N is a multiple of 6 and 5|N, and 4∤N. For N=180t, t odd, N=180t is divisible by 4? 180/4=45, so 180t is divisible by 4 if t is even? Actually 180=2^2*3^2*5, so 180t is divisible by 4 if t is even? Because 180 has factor 4? 180=4*45, yes, so 180 is divisible by 4. So if t is odd, 180t is divisible by 4? 180t = 4*45*t, so yes, it is divisible by 4 because 4 is a factor. So N=180t with t odd is divisible by 4. But we assumed 4∤N. So this subcase is contradictory: if 5|N and 4∤N, then N is not divisible by 4, but N=180t is divisible by 4 if t is any integer? Actually 180=2^2*3^2*5, so it has factor 2^2. So any multiple of 180 is divisible by 4. So N=180t is always divisible by 4. So the condition 4∤N cannot be satisfied for N that is a multiple of 180. Therefore, this subcase yields no solutions because N must be divisible by 30 and 5|N, but then N is divisible by 5 and 6, so N is divisible by 30. But if N is divisible by 30, then N is divisible by 2 and 3 and 5. For N to be divisible by 30, it is divisible by 2,3,5. But is it necessarily divisible by 4? Not necessarily; 30 is not divisible by 4. So N=30m, m integer. For N to be divisible by 30, m can be any integer. For N to be divisible by 4, we need 30m divisible by 4, i.e., 15m divisible by 2, so m even. So if m is odd, N is not divisible by 4. So N=30m with m odd gives N divisible by 30 but not by 4. So we need to check: In subcase B, we assumed 4∤N and 5|N. So N=30m with m odd. Then f(N)=31N/30 = 31m. So we need 31m ∈ F. For 31m to be a fixed point, we need 31m to be a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5. Since 31 is prime, 31m divisible by 2 requires m even. But m is odd, so 31m is odd, not divisible by 2. So 31m cannot be a multiple of 6. Thus, no solutions in this subcase. So subcase B yields no working numbers.

Subcase C: 4∤N and 5∤N. Then d3 = N/6. So f(N) = (5N/6) + N/6 = N. So f(N)=N. So N is a fixed point. So this is the case where N is a multiple of 6, not divisible by 4, not divisible by 5. So N ∈ F. So the fixed points themselves work.

Thus, for numbers that are multiples of 6, the only possibilities to work are either N ∈ F (fixed points) or N = 72t with t odd and 5∤t (which is a subcase of 4|N). But wait, we also need to consider numbers that are multiples of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5, i.e., fixed points. And numbers that are multiples of 6 and divisible by 4 but not by 5, and also such that f(N) is a fixed point. We derived that for N with 4|N, we need N divisible by 12, and then f(N)=13N/12. For f(N) to be a fixed point, we need 13N/12 ∈ F. That gave N=72t with t odd and 5∤t. So these are the numbers that map to fixed points. But also, could there be numbers that are multiples of 6 and divisible by 4 but not by 5 that are not of the form 72t? For example, N=24 is divisible by 4 and 6, but N=24=12*2, so N=12m with m=2. Then f(N)=13*2=26, which is not fixed. So N=24 fails. N=36 is divisible by 4? 36/4=9, yes, so N=12*3, m=3, f=39, not fixed. So N=36 fails. N=48 is divisible by 4, N=12*4, m=4, f=52, not fixed. N=60 is divisible by 4? 60/4=15, yes, N=12*5, m=5, f=65, not fixed. N=72 is 12*6, m=6, f=78, fixed. So indeed, only when m is a multiple of 6 (i.e., N=12*6t=72t) does f(N) become a fixed point. So the condition is that N is divisible by 72 and N/72 is odd and not divisible by 5. But note: For N=72t, t odd and 5∤t, we have N divisible by 12, and also N divisible by 4? Yes, because 72 is divisible by 4. So these numbers are in the subcase A.

Now, what about numbers that are multiples of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5? Those are fixed points, and they work. So the set of working numbers that are multiples of 6 is exactly: F ∪ {72t : t odd, 5∤t}. But note: Some numbers in F may also be of the form 72t? For example, 72 is 72*1, but 72 is not in F because it is divisible by 4? Actually 72 is divisible by 4, so it's not in F. So F numbers are not divisible by 4, so they cannot be of the form 72t because 72t is divisible by 4. So the two sets are disjoint.

So far, we have found many working numbers: all fixed points F, and numbers of the form 72t with t odd and 5∤t. Also, we have found that numbers like 72, 216, 504, 936, 648, 1944, 1368, 2916, etc., work. But are there any other numbers that work? For instance, 288 fails, 336 fails, 384 fails, 432 fails, 480 fails, 528 fails, 576 fails, 600 fails. So the pattern seems to be: numbers that are multiples of 72 and have no factor 5? Actually 72*5=360 fails because 5|360. But 72*7=504 works. So the condition for numbers of the form 72t to work is that t is odd and not divisible by 5. But also, could there be numbers that are not multiples of 72 but still work? For example, 6,18,42,54,66,78,102,114,126,138,162,174,186,198,222,234,246,258,282,294,306,342,354,366,378,402,414,426,432? Wait 432 fails. So the fixed points themselves are not multiples of 72 generally; they are numbers ≡ 6 mod 12 and not divisible by 5. So they are all multiples of 6 but not of 12? Actually 6 mod 12 means they are 6,18,30,42,54,66,78,... but 30 is divisible by 5, so not in F. So F numbers are multiples of 6 but not of 12? Check: 6 is not divisible by 12; 18 not; 42 not; 54 not; 66 not; 78 not; 102 not; 114 not; 126 not; 138 not; 162 not; 174 not; 186 not; 198 not; 222 not; 234 not; 246 not; 258 not; 282 not; 294 not; 306 not; 342 not; 354 not; 366 not; 378 not; 402 not; 414 not; 426 not; 438? 438=2*3*73, 438 mod 12 = 6? 438/12=36.5, remainder 6, so 438 is not divisible by 12. So indeed, fixed points are not divisible by 12 because they have only one factor 2 (since they are multiples of 6 but not of 4). So they are of the form 2*odd, so they are not divisible by 12 (which is 4*3). So F numbers are not multiples of 12. So the numbers that map to fixed points are multiples of 72, which are divisible by 12. So there are two disjoint families: F (fixed points) and Preimages of F (numbers that are multiples of 72 with odd quotient not divisible by 5). But are there numbers that are not multiples of 72 but still map to F? For example, consider N=2^a * 3^b * p with a≥3, b≥1? For N=2^3*3^2*5=360, we got f=390 not fixed. For N=2^3*3^2*7=504 works. So the condition for N to map to a fixed point is that f(N) is a fixed point. For N with 6|N and 4|N, we derived f(N)=13N/12. For this to be a fixed point, we need 13N/12 ∈ F. That gave N=72t with t odd and 5∤t. So these are the only numbers with 4|N that map to fixed points. Could there be numbers with 4∤N but 5|N that map to fixed points? We already considered that case and got f(N)=31N/30, which would be fixed only if 31N/30 is a fixed point. But for N divisible by 30 and not by 4, N=30m with m odd? Then f=31m. For 31m to be fixed, we need 31m multiple of 6, not divisible by 4 or 5. Since 31 is odd, m must be even to be multiple of 2, but m odd gives odd, so no. So no solutions.

What about numbers with 4∤N and 5∤N, i.e., fixed points themselves. So the only numbers that work are those in F and those of the form 72t with t odd and 5∤t.

But wait, we also have numbers like 72*1=72 works, 72*3=216 works, 72*5=360 fails, 72*7=504 works, 72*9=648 works, 72*11=792 works, 72*13=936 works, 72*15=1080 fails? 1080=72*15, t=15 odd, 5|15, so fails. 72*17=1224? 1224=72*17, t=17 odd not multiple of 5, should work. Let's test 1224: 1224=2^3*3^2*17? Actually 1224/8=153, 153=9*17, so yes. Then f(1224)=13*1224/12=13*102=1326. 1326=2*3*13*17? 1326/2=663, 663/3=221, 221=13*17, so 1326=2*3*13*17. Is 1326 a fixed point? Check: 1326/2=663, 1326/3=442, 1326/6=221. Are there any divisors larger than 221? Possibly 1326/4? Not integer. 1326/13=102? 102 <221. So order: 663,442,221 sum=1326. So 1326 is fixed. So 1224 works.

Thus, the set of working a1 is: all numbers N such that N is a multiple of 6 and either N ≡ 6 mod 12 (i.e., N/2 is odd) and 5∤N, or N is a multiple of 72 and N/72 is odd and not divisible by 5.

But note: N=6 is in F, N=18 in F, N=42 in F, etc. Also N=72 is in the second family. So the answer is: a1 can be any positive integer that is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, except that if it is divisible by 4, then it must be divisible by 72 and the quotient must be odd and not divisible by 5? Actually the condition "multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5" includes both families? For example, 30 is multiple of 6 and divisible by 5, so excluded. 36 is multiple of 6, not divisible by 5, but fails because it is divisible by 4? Actually 36 is not divisible by 4 (36/4=9, so 36 is divisible by 4? 4*9=36, yes, 36 is divisible by 4. So 36 is multiple of 6, not divisible by 5, but fails. So the condition "multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5" is not sufficient; we need to exclude numbers that are divisible by 4 but not by 72? Actually 36 is divisible by 4, but not by 72 (since 72/4=18, 36/4=9, so 36 is 4*9, 72 is 4*18). So the condition for numbers divisible by 4 is that they must be multiples of 72 and the quotient must be odd and not divisible by 5. But 36 is divisible by 4, but N/4=9, which is odd, and 9 is not divisible by 5, so why does 36 fail? Because for 36, f=39, which is not fixed. So the condition is not just N/4 odd; we need N to be divisible by 72. So the set of numbers that work is exactly: {N: N is a multiple of 6, and either (N/2 is odd and 5∤N) or (N is a multiple of 72 and N/72 is odd and 5∤N)}. But note that if N is a multiple of 72, then N/2 is even, so the first condition fails. So the two families are disjoint.

Now, we need to check if there are any numbers that are multiples of 6 but not divisible by 5 that are not in either family? For example, N=12 is multiple of 6, not divisible by 5, but 12 is divisible by 4? 12/4=3, yes, so it's divisible by 4, but not by 72. So it fails. N=24 fails. N=48 fails. N=60 fails (divisible by 5). N=84 fails (divisible by 4? 84/4=21, yes, but not by 72). So indeed, the only numbers that work are those in F and those that are multiples of 72 with odd quotient not divisible by 5.

But is it possible that a number that is not a multiple of 6 works? For numbers with at least two distinct primes, the three largest proper divisors are typically N/p_min, N/p_2 (where p_2 is the second smallest prime factor), and then either N/p_3 (third smallest prime factor) or N/p_min^2? Smallest three: 2,3,3. Then N/2=9, N/3=6, N/3=6 again? But we need distinct divisors; the third largest is 3, which is N/6? Not from prime factors. So pattern: The three largest proper divisors are N/p_min, N/p_2, and N/p_3? For 18, p_2=3 gives N/3=6, p_3=3 gives N/3=6 again, but we have 3 as third largest, which is N/6? Actually 6 is a divisor, and N/6=3, so 3 is the complementary divisor of 6. So the three largest are: N/p_min, N/p_2, and then the next largest divisor after those? It could be N/p_3 if p_3 is a prime factor distinct from p_min and p_2? But if p_3 = p_2, then N/p_2 is already taken, so the next largest divisor is N/(p_min * p_2) maybe? For 18, p_min=2, p_2=3, then N/(2*3)=3, which is the third largest. So in general, for numbers with at least two distinct prime factors, the three largest proper divisors are: N/p_min, N/p_2, and N/(p_min * p_2) if that is a proper divisor? But careful: For N=30, p_min=2, p_2=3, N/(2*3)=5, which is indeed the third largest (15,10,6? Wait 15,10,6: 6 is not 5. Actually 30: divisors: 1,2,3,5,6,10,15,30. Three largest: 15,10,6. Here N/(2*3)=5, but 5 is not among the three largest; 6 is larger than 5. So pattern is not that. For 30, the three largest are 15,10,6. 6 = N/5? Actually N/5=6, and 5 is a prime factor. So the third largest is N/p_3 where p_3 is the third smallest prime factor (5). So for 30, p_min=2, p_2=3, p_3=5. So the three largest are N/p_min, N/p_2, N/p_3. For 18, p_min=2, p_2=3, p_3=3? Actually 18=2*3^2, prime factors: 2,3,3. The three smallest prime factors (with multiplicity) are 2,3,3. Then N/2=9, N/3=6, N/3=6 again, but we need three distinct divisors; the third largest is 3, which is N/6? But N/6=3, and 6 is a divisor. So the three largest are 9,6,3. Here 3 = N/6 = N/(2*3). So the third largest is N/(p_min * p_2). For 18, p_min=2, p_2=3, product=6, N/6=3. For 30, p_min=2, p_2=3, product=6, N/6=5, but 5 is not among the three largest because 6 is larger than 5. So why does 30 have 6 as third largest? Because 6 is a divisor, and its complementary divisor is 5. But 6 is larger than 5. So the third largest is N/5 = 6, which is N/p_3. So the pattern depends on whether N/(p_min * p_2) is greater than N/p_3? Actually we need to compare the divisors: N/p_min, N/p_2, N/p_3, and also possibly N/(p_min^2) etc. For numbers with distinct primes, the divisors are N/p_i, N/(p_i p_j), etc. The largest proper divisor is N/p_min. The second largest is either N/p_2 or N/p_min^2? But if p_min^2 divides N, then N/p_min^2 is a divisor; compare N/p_2 and N/p_min^2: Since p_min is smallest, p_min^2 may be less than p_2? Actually p_min^2 could be less than p_2 if p_min=2 and p_2=3, then 4>3, so N/p_min^2 = N/4 is smaller than N/3? Actually N/4 < N/3, so N/p_2 is larger. So second largest is N/p_2 if p_2 is distinct prime and p_min^2 does not divide N? But if p_min^2 divides N, then N/p_min^2 is a divisor; compare N/p_2 and N/p_min^2: Since p_min^2 >= p_2? Not necessarily; p_min=2, p_2=5, then 4<5, so N/4 > N/5, so N/p_min^2 could be larger than N/p_2. So we need to consider all divisors. However, note that the three largest proper divisors are the three largest numbers d < N such that d|N. Since N is composite, the largest proper divisor is N/p_min. The second largest proper divisor is the maximum among divisors d < N such that d ≠ N/p_min. Typically, it will be either N/p_2 (if p_2 is a prime factor distinct from p_min) or N/p_min^2 (if p_min^2 divides N and p_min^2 < p_2? Actually compare N/p_min^2 and N/p_2: N/p_min^2 > N/p_2 iff p_min^2 < p_2. So if p_min^2 < p_2, then N/p_min^2 is larger than N/p_2; but N/p_min^2 is a divisor only if p_min^2 divides N, i.e., exponent of p_min >=2. So in that case, second largest is N/p_min^2. Then third largest? Possibly N/p_2 or N/p_min^3 etc. So the sum is not simply a function of the three smallest prime factors; it depends on the exponent structure.

But perhaps we can find a pattern or invariants. Let's denote s(N) = sum of the three largest proper divisors of N. We need to study the dynamics of N -> s(N). The condition that each a_n has at least three proper divisors ensures that s(N) is defined. We need to find all a1 such that the sequence is infinite (i.e., never hits a number with less than 3 proper divisors). Possibly the sequence eventually becomes periodic or constant. Let's explore small numbers.

We need to compute s(N) for composite N with at least 3 proper divisors. Let's list small N and s(N):

N=4: divisors:1,2,4. Proper:1,2. Only 2 proper divisors, so not allowed. So a1 cannot be 4 because a1 must have at least 3 proper divisors. Actually condition: "The infinite sequence a1, a2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors." So a1 must have at least 3 proper divisors. So a1 must be composite with at least 3 proper divisors. So a1 ∈ {6,8,9,10,12,14,15,16,18,20,21,22,24,25,26,27,28,30,...}. But also we need that a2 = s(a1) also has at least 3 proper divisors, etc.

We see that some numbers are fixed points: 6, 18, 42, 54, 66, 78, ... Also 6 is 2*3, 18=2*3^2, 42=2*3*7, 54=2*3^3, 66=2*3*11, 78=2*3*13. Also maybe 30? No 30 gave 31. 12 gave 13. So fixed points seem to be numbers of the form 2 * p * q? Not exactly: 6=2*3, 18=2*3^2, 42=2*3*7, 54=2*3^3, 66=2*3*11, 78=2*3*13. So all are multiples of 6. But 6 itself is 2*3. 18 is 2*3^2. 42 is 2*3*7. 54 is 2*3^3. 66 is 2*3*11. 78 is 2*3*13. So maybe all numbers of the form 2 * 3^k? 6 (k=1), 18 (k=2), 54 (k=3). But 42,66,78 are not powers of 3 times 2; they are 2*3*7, 2*3*11, 2*3*13. So they are 2*3*p where p is a prime >3? But 2*3*5=30 gave 31 not fixed. So p=5 fails. 2*3*7=42 works. 2*3*11=66 works. 2*3*13=78 works. What about 2*3*17=102? Let's test 102: divisors: 1,2,3,6,17,34,51,102. Proper: 1,2,3,6,17,34,51. Three largest: 51,34,17 sum=102. So 102 is fixed! So 2*3*p with p prime >3? But p=5 fails, p=7 works, p=11 works, p=13 works, p=17 works. Why does p=5 fail? Because for 30, the three largest proper divisors are 15,10,6 sum=31, not 30. So 30 is not fixed. So what's special about p=5? For 2*3*5=30, the divisors: 1,2,3,5,6,10,15,30. The three largest proper: 15,10,6 sum=31. So 30 is not fixed. For 2*3*7=42, divisors: 1,2,3,6,7,14,21,42. Three largest: 21,14,7 sum=42. So fixed. For 2*3*11=66, divisors: 1,2,3,6,11,22,33,66. Three largest: 33,22,11 sum=66. So fixed. So why does 30 differ? Because 5 is a prime factor, but 6 is also a divisor? Actually note: For 30, the three largest proper divisors are 15,10,6. But 6 is not a prime factor; it's 2*3. However, 15 and 10 are N/p for p=2 and p=3. The third largest is 6, which is N/5? Actually N/5=6, so it's N/p for p=5. So the three largest are N/2, N/3, N/5. That's typical when the number has three distinct prime factors. For 42, the three largest are 21,14,7 which are N/2, N/3, N/7. So that's also N/p for each prime factor. For 66, N/2=33, N/3=22, N/11=6? Wait N/11=6, but 6 is not the third largest; the third largest is 11? Actually 66: divisors: 1,2,3,6,11,22,33,66. Three largest: 33,22,11 sum=66. So here the third largest is 11, which is N/6? Actually N/6=11, and 6 is a divisor. So the three largest are N/2, N/3, and N/6? But N/6=11, which is a prime factor? 11 is prime. So the three largest are N/2, N/3, and N/6. But N/6 is not N/p for a prime p; it's N/(2*3). So the pattern for 42: N/2=21, N/3=14, N/7=6? Wait N/7=6, but 6 is not among the three largest; the three largest are 21,14,7. So here 7 is N/6? Actually N/6=7, and 6 is a divisor. So the three largest are N/2, N/3, and N/6. For 66, N/2=33, N/3=22, N/6=11, which is prime. So the three largest are N/2, N/3, N/6. For 30, N/2=15, N/3=10, N/6=5, but 5 is not among the three largest; the three largest are 15,10,6. So why does 6 appear? Because 6 is N/5, and 5 is a prime factor. So for 30, the three largest are N/2, N/3, N/5. So the third largest is N/p for the largest prime factor? Actually 5 is the largest prime factor, and N/5=6 is larger than N/6=5. So the three largest are N/2, N/3, and N/5. For 42, N/2=21, N/3=14, N/7=6, but N/6=7, so 7 > 6, so the third largest is 7 (N/6), not 6. So the third largest is N/6. So it depends on whether N/(p_min * p_2) is greater than N/p_3. Since p_min=2, p_2=3, product=6. Then N/6 is a divisor. Compare N/6 and N/p_3: N/6 > N/p_3 iff 6 < p_3. So if p_3 > 6, then N/6 is larger than N/p_3, so the three largest are N/2, N/3, N/6. If p_3 < 6, then N/p_3 > N/6, so the three largest are N/2, N/3, N/p_3. But p_3 is the third smallest prime factor? For 30, p_3=5 (<6), so N/5=6 > N/6=5, so third largest is N/5. For 42, p_3=7 (>6), so N/6=7 > N/7=6, so third largest is N/6. So the fixed point condition s(N)=N becomes: N/2 + N/3 + N/6 = N? That gives N/2+N/3+N/6 = N*(3+2+1)/6 = N, so indeed if the three largest are N/2, N/3, N/6, then sum = N. So numbers where the three largest proper divisors are N/2, N/3, N/6 are fixed points. Now, we need to find all a1 that lead to an infinite sequence. That is, the orbit never hits a number with less than 3 proper divisors. Since we have found many numbers that work (fixed points and some preimages), we need to determine the complete set.

Observation: The process is defined by a_{n+1} = sum of three largest proper divisors of a_n. Let's denote f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N. For N with at least 3 proper divisors, f(N) is defined. We need to find all N such that the sequence N, f(N), f(f(N)), ... never hits a number with less than 3 proper divisors. This is like the set of numbers that are in the "basin of attraction" of the fixed points (or maybe other cycles). We have found fixed points F = {6k: k odd, 5 ∤ k}. Also we have numbers that map to these fixed points, like 72, 216, 648, etc. Are there numbers that map to these fixed points after more than one step? For example, 72 maps to 78 (fixed). 216 maps to 234 (fixed). 648 maps to 702 (fixed). So these are preimages of fixed points. Could there be numbers that map to these preimages? For instance, is there a number N such that f(N)=72? Then N would be a preimage of 72, and then the sequence would be N,72,78,78,... So N would work if f(N)=72 and 72 works. So we need to find all N such that f(N)=72. Similarly, for 216, etc. So we need to characterize the set of numbers that eventually reach a fixed point. Possibly all numbers that are multiples of 6? But 30 fails. So maybe numbers that are not divisible by 5? But 12 is divisible by 3 and 4, and fails. So it's more subtle.

Let's try to analyze f(N) more systematically. For a positive integer N, let its prime factorization be N = ∏ p_i^{e_i}. The divisors are all numbers of the form ∏ p_i^{a_i} with 0 ≤ a_i ≤ e_i. The largest proper divisor is N/p_min where p_min is the smallest prime factor. The second largest proper divisor: we need to consider the next largest divisor less than N. Typically, it will be N/p_2 where p_2 is the second smallest prime factor (if p_min^2 does not divide N) or N/p_min^2 if p_min^2 divides N and p_min^2 < p_2? But careful: For numbers with repeated prime factors, the divisors can be N/p_min^2, N/p_min^3, etc. The three largest proper divisors are the three largest numbers d < N such that d|N. Since the divisors are symmetric: if d divides N, then N/d also divides N. The largest proper divisor is N/p_min. The second largest is either N/p_2 (if p_2 is the second smallest prime factor) or N/p_min^2 (if p_min^2 divides N and p_min^2 < p_2? Actually compare N/p_min^2 and N/p_2: N/p_min^2 > N/p_2 iff p_min^2 < p_2. Since p_min is the smallest prime, p_min^2 could be less than p_2 if p_min=2 and p_2=5? 4<5, so N/4 > N/5. So if N is divisible by 4, then N/4 is a divisor and is larger than N/5. So second largest could be N/4. But also N/3 is larger than N/4? Actually N/3 > N/4. So order: N/2 > N/3 > N/4 > N/5 > N/6 ... So the second largest is N/3 if 3|N, else N/4 if 4|N and 3∤N? Actually if N is divisible by 2 and 3, then N/2 and N/3 are both divisors, and N/2 > N/3. So second largest is N/3. If N is divisible by 2 but not by 3, then the next largest divisor could be N/4 if 4|N, or N/5 if 5|N? But also N/2 is largest, so we need to compare N/4 and N/5: N/4 > N/5. So if N is divisible by 4, then N/4 is a divisor and is > N/5. So second largest is N/4. If N is divisible by 2 but not by 4, then N/2 is largest, and the next largest divisor could be N/3 if 3|N? But if 3∤N, then the next candidate is N/5 if 5|N, etc. But also N/ (p) for p>2. So the three largest proper divisors are: the three largest numbers among {N/p : p is a prime factor of N} ∪ {N/p^2 : p^2|N} ∪ {N/p^3 : p^3|N} ... but note that N/p^2 is a divisor if p^2|N. However, N/p^2 may be less than N/p for p>1. So the three largest are typically: N/p_min, then either N/p_2 or N/p_min^2, then the next largest among the remaining.

But we can compute f(N) by considering the set of divisors. There is known concept: For a composite number N, the sum of the three largest proper divisors is often N + something? Not sure.

Maybe we can find an invariant or a property that the sequence eventually becomes constant. Let's explore further.

From our experiments, the fixed points are numbers N such that N ≡ 6 mod 12 and N not divisible by 5. Also, we found that numbers like 72, 216, 648, etc., which are 2^3 * 3^k (k≥2), map to fixed points. Also, maybe numbers like 2^3 * 3^2 * something? 72 works, 216 works, 648 works. What about 2^3 * 3^2 * 5 = 360? Let's test 360: divisors: 1,2,3,4,5,6,8,9,10,12,15,18,20,24,30,36,40,45,60,72,90,120,180,360. Proper: 1,2,3,4,5,6,8,9,10,12,15,18,20,24,30,36,40,45,60,72,90,120,180. Maybe we need to analyze the function f(N) more deeply. Let's denote the three largest proper divisors of N as d1 > d2 > d3. Then f(N) = d1+d2+d3. Since d1 = N/p_min, and d2, d3 are some divisors. Actually no, f(N) is a fixed point, so f(f(N)) = f(N). So the sequence becomes constant after that step. So we need to find all N such that f(N) is in F, and also N itself has at least 3 proper divisors. But also we need that the process never fails before reaching F. That is, for every step, the number has at least 3 proper divisors. So if N is such that f(N) is in F, then the sequence will be N, f(N), f(N), ... So as long as N has at least 3 proper divisors, and f(N) has at least 3 proper divisors (which it does, since F numbers have at least 3 proper divisors? Also, if N is itself in F, then it's a fixed point, so works. So the set of possible a1 is exactly the set of numbers N such that f(N) is in F (including N in F). But wait: Could there be numbers that map to a fixed point after more than one step, but the intermediate numbers are not in F? For example, suppose N -> M -> F, where M is not in F but f(M) is in F. Then N works if f(N)=M and M works. So we need to find all N such that f(N) is in the set S that eventually maps to F. That is, the set of numbers whose orbit eventually lands in F. So we need to characterize the basin of attraction of F under f.

Thus, the problem reduces to: Determine all positive integers N such that the sequence defined by a_{n+1}=f(a_n) is infinite (i.e., every a_n has at least 3 proper divisors). Since f is defined for numbers with at least 3 proper divisors, we need that for all n, a_n is in the domain of f. So we need to find all a1 such that the orbit stays in the domain.

We need to find all a1 such that the orbit never hits a number with less than 3 proper divisors. Since we have found many numbers that work, we need to characterize them.

Let's try to analyze the function f(N) more systematically. For a given N, let its prime factorization be N = ∏ p_i^{e_i}. The set of divisors is symmetric: d and N/d. The largest proper divisor is N/p_min. The second largest proper divisor: we need to consider the next largest divisor less than N. Since divisors come in pairs, the largest proper divisor is N/p_min. The next largest proper divisor could be either N/p_2 (if p_2 is the second smallest prime factor) or N/p_min^2 (if p_min^2 divides N and p_min^2 < p_2? Actually compare N/p_min^2 and N/p_2: N/p_min^2 > N/p_2 iff p_min^2 < p_2. Since p_min is the smallest prime, p_min^2 could be less than p_2 if p_min=2 and p_2=5? 4<5, so yes. So if N is divisible by 4, then N/4 is a divisor and is larger than N/5. So the second largest divisor is N/3 if 3|N, else N/4 if 4|N and 3∤N, else N/5 if 5|N, etc. But also if N has a prime factor p such that p_min^2 divides N, then N/p_min^2 is a divisor, but it might be less than N/p_2 if p_min^2 > p_2. For example, N=12=2^2*3, p_min=2, p_2=3, N/2=6, N/3=4, N/4=3. So second largest is 4 (N/3). So indeed, the second largest is N/p_2 if p_2 exists (i.e., N has at least 2 distinct primes) and p_2 < p_min^2? Actually p_min^2=4, p_2=3, so 3<4, so N/3 > N/4. So second largest is N/p_2. For N=18=2*3^2, p_min=2, p_2=3, N/2=9, N/3=6, N/6=3. So second largest is 6 (N/3). For N=20=2^2*5, p_min=2, p_2=5, N/2=10, N/4=5, N/5=4. So second largest is 5? Actually divisors:1,2,4,5,10,20. Proper:1,2,4,5,10. Three largest:10,5,4 sum=19. So second largest is 5 (N/4? Wait N/4=5, N/5=4, so second largest is N/4). So here p_2=5, but N/4=5, and N/5=4. So the second largest is N/4, not N/5. So the order: N/2=10, then N/4=5, then N/5=4. So second largest is N/4. So the second largest divisor is N/p where p is the smallest prime factor such that p^2 divides N? Actually p_min=2, and 2^2=4 divides N, so N/4 is a divisor. Compare N/4 and N/5: N/4 > N/5 because 4<5. So second largest is N/4. So the second largest is N/p_min^2 if p_min^2 divides N and p_min^2 < p_2? But here p_2=5, and p_min^2=4 <5, so yes. So the pattern: The three largest proper divisors are: d1 = N/p_min, d2 = max( N/p_2, N/p_min^2 ) if both exist, else the larger of the two, and d3 = the next largest among the remaining. But we can compute f(N) by considering the set of divisors.

Maybe there is a known result: For a composite number N, the sum of its three largest proper divisors is equal to N + something? Let's try to compute f(N) for N that are multiples of 6 but not fixed. So why do some numbers work? Let's examine the numbers that work: 72, 216, 504, 936, 648, 2916, etc. These are all numbers that are divisible by 8 and also by 9? 72=8*9, 216=8*27, 504=8*63, 936=8*117, 648=8*81. Maybe the condition is that a1 is not divisible by 5? But 12 is not divisible by 5 and fails. So no.

So all working numbers we have found are multiples of 6. But not all multiples of 6 work. Which multiples of 6 work? Those that are in F (fixed points) and those that map to F. Let's compute f(N) for N=2^3 * 3^2 * p. We have N = 8*9*p = 72p. For p=7, N=504, f=546. For p=13, N=936, f= ? 936=8*9*13=72*13=936. For N=72p, f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = N*13/12 = 72p*13/12 = 78p. So f(N)=78p. So we need 78p ∈ F. That is, 78p must be a fixed point. 78p = 6*13p. For 78p to be in F, we need 78p not divisible by 4 and not divisible by 5. Since 78p = 2*3*13*p, it is divisible by 2 but not by 4 unless p=2? Actually 78p = 2*3*13*p. For it to be divisible by 4, we need p even, but p is prime >3, so p odd, so 78p is not divisible by 4. So condition reduces to: 78p not divisible by 5. That is, p ≠ 5. So indeed, for N=72p with p prime >3, p≠5, we have f(N)=78p, which is fixed. So these numbers work. So the set of numbers of the form 2^3 * 3^2 * p with p prime >3, p≠5, work. But also numbers of the form 2^3 * 3^k for k≥2 work? Let's test N=2^3 * 3^k = 8*3^k. For k=2, N=72 works (f=78). For k=3, N=216 works (f=234). For k=4, N=648 works (f=702). For k=5, N=1944 works (f=2106). So f(N)=? For N=8*3^k, divisors: N/2=4*3^k, N/3= (8/3)*3^k? Actually N/3= (8*3^k)/3 = 8*3^{k-1}. N/4=2*3^k. So the three largest proper divisors: 4*3^k, 8*3^{k-1}, 2*3^k? But we need to compare 8*3^{k-1} and 2*3^k. Since 8*3^{k-1} = (8/3)*3^k, and 2*3^k = 2*3^k. For k≥2, 8/3 ≈ 2.666 > 2, so 8*3^{k-1} > 2*3^k. So order: 4*3^k, 8*3^{k-1}, 2*3^k. Their sum = 4*3^k + 8*3^{k-1} + 2*3^k = 6*3^k + 8*3^{k-1} = 6*3^k + (8/3)*3^k = (6 + 8/3)*3^k = (18/3+8/3)*3^k = (26/3)*3^k = (26/3)*3^k = 26*3^{k-1}. So f(N)=26*3^{k-1}. For k=2, 26*3=78, fixed. For k=3, 26*9=234, fixed. For k=4, 26*27=702, fixed. For k=5, 26*81=2106, fixed. So indeed, f(N)=26*3^{k-1} is a fixed point because it's 2*13*3^{k-1} and 13 is not 5, and it's not divisible by 4 (since 2*13*3^{k-1} has only one factor 2). So these work.

Now, what about numbers of the form 2^3 * 3^2 * p * q? Possibly they might also work if the f-image is a fixed point. But we need to find all a1 such that the orbit is infinite. This is a problem of determining the basin of attraction of the fixed points. Perhaps the answer is: All positive integers that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 5? But we have counterexample: 36 is multiple of 6, not divisible by 5, fails. So not that.

Maybe the answer is: All positive integers that are not prime and not of the form 2^a * 3^b * p where p is a prime? That seems too complicated.

Let's try to analyze the function f(N) more systematically. We can try to compute f(N) in terms of N's prime factorization. Let N = ∏ p_i^{e_i}. The divisors are all numbers of the form ∏ p_i^{a_i} with 0 ≤ a_i ≤ e_i. The largest proper divisor is N/p_min. The second largest proper divisor: we need to consider the next largest divisor less than N. Since the divisors are symmetric, the largest proper divisor is N/p_min. The next largest divisor could be either N/p_2 (if p_2 exists and p_2 < p_min^2? Actually compare N/p_2 and N/p_min^2: N/p_2 > N/p_min^2 iff p_2 < p_min^2. So if p_min^2 ≤ p_2, then the second largest is N/p_min^2? But wait, if p_min^2 divides N, then N/p_min^2 is a divisor. But also N/p_2 is a divisor if p_2 is a prime factor. If p_min^2 > p_2, then N/p_2 > N/p_min^2, so second largest is N/p_2. If p_min^2 ≤ p_2, then N/p_min^2 ≥ N/p_2? Actually if p_min^2 ≤ p_2, then N/p_min^2 ≥ N/p_2, so the second largest is N/p_min^2. But also there could be N/p_min^3, etc. So the three largest proper divisors are: d1 = N/p_min, d2 = max( N/p_2, N/p_min^2 ) (if both exist), and d3 = the next largest among the remaining divisors. However, we need to consider all divisors, not just those from prime factors. For numbers with repeated prime factors, there are divisors like N/p_min^2, N/p_min^3, etc. So the three largest are often: N/p_min, then either N/p_2 or N/p_min^2, then the next largest among the remaining. But we can compute f(N) as N/p_min + N/p_2 + N/p_min^2? Not always; for N=12, p_min=2, p_2=3, N/2=6, N/3=4, N/4=3. So the three largest are 6,4,3. That's N/2, N/3, N/4. So d2 = N/3, d3 = N/4. Here N/4 = N/p_min^2. So f=6+4+3=13. For N=18, p_min=2, p_2=3, N/2=9, N/3=6, N/6=3. So d2=6, d3=3. That's N/2, N/3, N/6. So f=9+6+3=18. For N=20, p_min=2, p_2=5, N/2=10, N/4=5, N/5=4. So d2=5, d3=4. That's N/2, N/4, N/5. So f=10+5+4=19. For N=30, p_min=2, p_2=3, p_3=5, N/2=15, N/3=10, N/5=6. So d2=10, d3=6. That's N/2, N/3, N/5. So f=15+10+6=31. For N=36, p_min=2, p_2=3, N/2=18, N/3=12, N/4=9? So 582 fixed. 583? 583=11*53, proper:1,11,53. Three largest:53,11,1 sum=65 fails. 584 fails. 585 fails. 586 fails. 588 fails. 590 fails. 591? 591=3*197, proper:1,3,197. Three largest:197,3,1 sum=201. f(591)=201. 201=3*67, proper:1,3,67. Three largest:67,3,1 sum=71. f(201)=71 prime fails. So 591 fails. 592 fails. 594 fails. 595 fails. 596 fails. 597? 597=3*199, proper:1,3,199. Three largest:199,3,1 sum=203. f(597)=203. 203=7*29, proper:1,7,29. Three largest:29,7,1 sum=37 fails. So 597 fails. 598 fails. 600? 600=2^3*3*5^2, proper:1,2,3,4,5,6,8,10,12,15,20,24,25,30,40,50,60,75,100,120,150,200,300. Three largest:300,200,150 sum=650. f(600)=650. 650=2*5^2*13, proper:1,2,5,10,13,25,26,50,65,130,325. Three largest:325,130,65 sum=520. f(650)=520. 520=2^3*5*13, proper:1,2,4,5,8,10,13,20,26,40,52,65,104,130,260. Three largest:260,130,104 sum=494. f(520)=494->? 494=2*13*19, proper:1,2,13,19,26,38,247. Three largest:247,38,26 sum=311. f(494)=311 prime fails. So 600 fails.

So far, we have found many working numbers: all fixed points F, and numbers of the form 72t with t odd and 5∤t. Also, we have found that numbers like 72, 216, 504, 936, 648, 1944, 1368, 2916, etc., work. But are there any other numbers that work? For instance, 288 fails, 336 fails, 384 fails, 432 fails, 480 fails, 528 fails, 576 fails, 600 fails. So the pattern seems to be: numbers that are multiples of 72 and have no factor 5? Actually 72*5=360 fails because 5|360. But 72*7=504 works. So the condition for numbers of the form 72t to work is that t is odd and not divisible by 5. But also, could there be numbers that are not multiples of 72 but still work? For example, 6,18,42,54,66,78,102,114,126,138,162,174,186,198,222,234,246,258,282,294,306,342,354,366,378,402,414,426,432? Wait 432 fails. So the fixed points themselves are not multiples of 72 generally; they are numbers ≡ 6 mod 12 and not divisible by 5. So they are all multiples of 6 but not of 12? Actually 6 mod 12 means they are 6,18,30,42,54,66,78,... but 30 is divisible by 5, so not in F. So F numbers are multiples of 6 but not of 12? Check: 6 is not divisible by 12; 18 not; 42 not; 54 not; 66 not; 78 not; 102 not; 114 not; 126 not; 138 not; 162 not; 174 not; 186 not; 198 not; 222 not; 234 not; 246 not; 258 not; 282 not; 294 not; 306 not; 342 not; 354 not; 366 not; 378 not; 402 not; 414 not; 426 not; 438? 438=2*3*73, 438 mod 12 = 6? 438/12=36.5, remainder 6, so 438 is not divisible by 12. So indeed, fixed points are not divisible by 12 because they have only one factor 2 (since they are multiples of 6 but not of 4). So they are of the form 2*odd, so they are not divisible by 12 (which is 4*3). So F numbers are not multiples of 12. So the numbers that map to fixed points are multiples of 72, which are divisible by 12. So there are two disjoint families: F (fixed points) and Preimages of F (numbers that are multiples of 72 with odd quotient not divisible by 5). But are there numbers that are not multiples of 72 but still map to F? For example, consider N=2^a * 3^b * p with a≥3, b≥1? For N=2^3*3^2*5=360, we got f=390 not fixed. For N=2^3*3^2*7=504 works. So the condition for N to map to a fixed point is that f(N) is a fixed point. For N with 6|N and 4|N, we derived f(N)=13N/12. For this to be a fixed point, we need 13N/12 ∈ F. That gave N=72t with t odd and 5∤t. So these are the only numbers with 4|N that map to fixed points. Could there be numbers with 4∤N but 5|N that map to fixed points? We already considered that case and got f(N)=31N/30, which would be fixed only if 31N/30 is a fixed point. But for N divisible by 30 and not by 4, N=30m with m odd? Then f=31m. For 31m to be fixed, we need 31m multiple of 6, not divisible by 4 or 5. Since 31 is odd, m must be even to be multiple of 2, but m odd gives odd, so no. So no solutions.

What about numbers with 4∤N and 5∤N, i.e., fixed points themselves. So the only numbers that work are those in F and those of the form 72t with t odd and 5∤t.

But wait, we also have numbers like 72*1=72 works, 72*3=216 works, 72*5=360 fails, 72*7=504 works, 72*9=648 works, 72*11=792 works, 72*13=936 works, 72*15=1080 fails? 1080=72*15, t=15 odd, 5|15, so fails. 72*17=1224? 1224=72*17, t=17 odd not multiple of 5, should work. Let's test 1224: 1224=2^3*3^2*17? Actually 1224/8=153, 153=9*17, so yes. Then f(1224)=13*1224/12=13*102=1326. 1326=2*3*13*17? 1326/2=663, 663/3=221, 221=13*17, so 1326=2*3*13*17. Is 1326 a fixed point? Check: 1326/2=663, 1326/3=442, 1326/6=221. Are there any divisors larger than 221? Possibly 1326/4? Not integer. 1326/13=102? 102 <221. So order: 663,442,221 sum=1326. So 1326 is fixed. So 1224 works.

Thus, the set of working a1 is: all numbers N such that N is a multiple of 6 and either N ≡ 6 mod 12 (i.e., N/2 is odd) and 5∤N, or N is a multiple of 72 and N/72 is odd and not divisible by 5.

But note: N=6 is in F, N=18 in F, N=42 in F, etc. Also N=72 is in the second family. So the answer is: a1 can be any positive integer that is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, except that if it is divisible by 4, then it must be divisible by 72 and the quotient must be odd and not divisible by 5? Actually the condition "multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5" includes both families? For example, 30 is multiple of 6 and divisible by 5, so excluded. 36 is multiple of 6, not divisible by 5, but fails because it is divisible by 4? Actually 36 is not divisible by 4 (36/4=9, so 36 is divisible by 4? 4*9=36, yes, 36 is divisible by 4. So 36 is multiple of 6, not divisible by 5, but fails. So the condition "multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5" is not sufficient; we need to exclude numbers that are divisible by 4 but not by 72? Actually 36 is divisible by 4, but not by 72 (since 72/4=18, 36/4=9, so 36 is 4*9, 72 is 4*18). So the condition for numbers divisible by 4 is that they must be multiples of 72 and the quotient must be odd and not divisible by 5. But 36 is divisible by 4, but N/4=9, which is odd, and 9 is not divisible by 5, so why does 36 fail? Because for 36, f=39, which is not fixed. So the condition is not just N/4 odd; we need N to be divisible by 72. So the set of numbers that work is exactly: {N: N is a multiple of 6, and either (N/2 is odd and 5∤N) or (N is a multiple of 72 and N/72 is odd and 5∤N)}. But note that if N is a multiple of 72, then N/2 is even, so the first condition fails. So the two families are disjoint.

Now, we need to check if there are any numbers that are multiples of 6 but not divisible by 5 that are not in either family? For example, N=12 is multiple of 6, not divisible by 5, but 12 is divisible by 4? 12/4=3, yes, so it's divisible by 4, but not by 72. So it fails. N=24 fails. N=48 fails. N=60 fails (divisible by 5). N=84 fails (divisible by 4? 84/4=21, yes, but not by 72). So indeed, the only numbers that work are those in F and those that are multiples of 72 with odd quotient not divisible by 5.

But is it possible that a number that is not a multiple of 6 works? <solution>
We were unable to produce a complete proof. However, the strongest partial progress is as follows:
- The process is well-defined for numbers with at least three proper divisors.
- Fixed points are numbers N such that N ≡ 6 mod 12 and 5 ∤ N.
- Numbers of the form 72t with t odd and 5 ∤ t map to fixed points.
- The sequence for a1 = 6, 18, 42, 54, 66, 78, 102, 114, 126, 138, 162, 174, 186, 198, 222, 234, 246, 258, 282, 294, 306, 342, 354, 366, 378, 402, 414, 426, 432? Actually 432 fails, so only those in F and those of the form 72t with t odd and 5 ∤ t.

But we cannot complete the rigorous proof. The problem likely requires a complete characterization. The answer is probably: a1 is any positive integer that is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, except that if it is divisible by 4, then it must be divisible by 72 and the quotient must be odd and not divisible by 5. However, we need to confirm if any numbers not multiples of 6 work. We tested many and none worked. So the set is: all numbers N such that N is a multiple of 6, and either N/2 is odd (i.e., N ≡ 6 mod 12) and 5 ∤ N, or N is a multiple of 72 and N/72 is odd and 5 ∤ N. This can be expressed as: a1 is any positive integer that is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, with the additional condition that if it is divisible by 4, then it must be divisible by 72. But note: 30 is multiple of 6 and divisible by 5, fails. 36 is multiple of 6, not divisible by 5, but divisible by 4, fails because not divisible by 72. So the condition is: a1 is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, and if it is divisible by 4, then it must be divisible by 72. This is equivalent to: a1 is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, and either a1 ≡ 6 mod 12 or a1 ≡ 0 mod 72. But 0 mod 72 includes numbers like 72, 144, 216, 288, 360, 432, 504, ... Among these, only those with quotient odd (i.e., 72*odd) work because 72*even gives divisibility by 16? Actually 144 is 72*2, quotient even, fails. So the condition is: a1 is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, and either a1 ≡ 6 mod 12 or a1 ≡ 72 mod 144? Because 72*odd ≡ 72 mod 144? 72*1=72, 72*3=216, 216 mod 144 = 72, 72*5=360 mod 144 = 72, so yes, numbers ≡ 72 mod 144 with odd quotient? Actually 72 mod 144 includes 72, 216, 360, 504, ... All these have quotient odd? 72=72*1, 216=72*3, 360=72*5, 504=72*7, so yes, they are all 72*odd. So the condition is: a1 is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, and either a1 ≡ 6 mod 12 or a1 ≡ 72 mod 144. But note: numbers ≡ 6 mod 12 include 6, 18, 30, 42, 54, 66, 78, 90, 102, 114, 126, 138, 150, 162, 174, 186, 198, 210, 222, 234, 246, 258, 270, 282, 294, 306, 318, 330, 342, 354, 366, 378, 390, 402, 414, 426, 438, 450, 462, 474, 486, 498, 510, 522, 534, 546, 558, 570, 582, 594, 606, 618, 630, 642, 654, 666, 678, 690, 702, 714, 726, 738, 750, 762, 774, 786, 798, 810, 822, 834, 846, 858, 870, 882, 894, 906, 918, 930, 942, 954, 966, 978, 990, 1002, ... Among these, those divisible by 5 are excluded. So the set is: all numbers N such that N is a multiple of 6, 5 ∤ N, and either N ≡ 6 mod 12 or N ≡ 72 mod 144. This can be expressed as: N is a multiple of 6, 5 ∤ N, and N/6 is odd and not divisible by 5? Actually N/6 odd implies N ≡ 6 mod 12? Because N=6*(odd) => N mod 12 = 6 if odd is odd? 6*1=6, 6*3=18≡6, 6*5=30≡6, so yes, N ≡ 6 mod 12. So N/6 odd is equivalent to N ≡ 6 mod 12. So the condition "N/6 odd" is the same as N ≡ 6 mod 12. But we also have numbers like 72, 216, 504, ... which are 6*12, 6*36, 6*84, ... So N/6 = 12, 36, 84, ... These are all multiples of 12? 12=12*1, 36=12*3, 84=12*7, so N/6 = 12 * odd. So N = 72 * odd. So the condition can be: N is a multiple of 6, 5 ∤ N, and N/6 is either odd or 12 times an odd number. That is, N/6 is not divisible by 5, and if it is even, then it must be divisible by 12. This is equivalent to: N/6 is not divisible by 5, and if it is divisible by 2, then it is divisible by 12. But note: N/6 even implies N/6 = 2k, then N=12k, so N divisible by 12. So the condition is: N is a multiple of 6, 5 ∤ N, and if N is divisible by 12, then N/12 is odd and not divisible by 5? Actually if N divisible by 12, then N=12m, and N/6=2m, so N/6 even. The condition that N/6 is 12 times an odd number means 2m = 12 * odd => m = 6 * odd. So N=12*6*odd=72*odd. So the condition is: N is a multiple of 6, 5 ∤ N, and if N is divisible by 12, then N/12 is a multiple of 6? Actually N/12 = odd*6? So N/12 is divisible by 6. So the condition can be: N is a multiple of 6, 5 ∤ N, and if N is divisible by 12, then N/12 is divisible by 6. That is, N/12 is a multiple of 6. So N is a multiple of 72. So the condition is: N is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, and either N is not divisible by 12, or N is divisible by 72. This is a clean characterization.

We have verified many examples. However, we have not proven that these are all possible. The problem likely expects a rigorous proof. Since we cannot complete the proof, we will output the partial solution and note that the answer is: all positive integers a1 such that a1 is a multiple of 6, a1 is not divisible by 5, and if a1 is divisible by 12, then a1 is divisible by 72. But we earlier tested 432 and it failed. So our condition "if N is divisible by 12, then N is divisible by 72" is necessary but not sufficient? Because 432 is divisible by 12 and by 72, but it fails. So we need to refine: N/6 must be either odd or 12 times an odd number. For 432, N/6=72, which is 12*6, but 6 is even, not odd. So the condition is: N/6 is either odd or 12 times an odd number. That is, N/6 = odd or N/6 = 12 * odd. So N/6 must be odd or 12 times an odd. For 432, N/6=72=12*6, but 6 is even, so it's not 12 times an odd. So the condition is: N/6 is not divisible by 5, and if it is even, then it must be divisible by 12 and the quotient must be odd. That is, N/6 = 2^k * m, and if k≥1, then 2^k must be exactly 12? Actually 12=2^2*3, so N/6 must be of the form 12 * odd. So N/6 must be a multiple of 12, and the factor after dividing by 12 must be odd. So N/6 = 12 * odd. So N = 72 * odd. So the condition is: N is a multiple of 6, 5 ∤ N, and either N/6 is odd or N/6 is 12 times an odd number. This is equivalent to: N is a multiple of 6, 5 ∤ N, and if N/6 is even, then N/6 is divisible by 12 and the quotient is odd. So N/6 must be of the form 2^a * b, and if a≥1, then 2^a must be exactly 12? But 12=2^2*3, so a must be at least 2, and the factor 3 must be present. So N/6 must be divisible by 12, and after dividing by 12, the result must be odd. So N/6 = 12 * odd. So N = 72 * odd. So the condition is: N is a multiple of 6, 5 ∤ N, and either N/6 is odd or N/6 is 12 times an odd number. This is a precise characterization. We have verified many examples: 72 works (72/6=12=12*1, odd quotient 1), 216 works (216/6=36=12*3, odd quotient 3), 504 works (504/6=84=12*7, odd quotient 7), 432 fails (432/6=72=12*6, odd quotient 6? 6 is even, so fails). So the condition is: N/6 is not divisible by 5, and if it is even, then it is divisible by 12 and the quotient is odd. This is equivalent to: N is a multiple of 6, 5 ∤ N, and N/6 is either odd or 12 times an odd number. This can be expressed as: N = 6k where k is a positive integer, 5 ∤ k, and if k is even, then k is a multiple of 12 and k/12 is odd. So k = 12 * odd or k = odd. So the set is: all numbers N such that N/6 is an integer not divisible by 5, and either N/6 is odd or N/6 is 12 times an odd number.
